<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="the-neuromuscular-system">The Neuromuscular System<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-neuromuscular-system" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The neuromuscular system may be affected leading to:</p>
<p><strong>Muscular Weakness or Paralysis</strong> - from lead, arsenic, botulism poison, hemlock, organic mercurials, thallium, DDT, shellfish, carbon disulfide.</p>
<p><strong>Muscle Fasciculation's</strong> - due to phosphate ester and other insecticides, nicotine, black widow spider, scorpions, manganese, shellfish.</p>
<p><strong>Muscle Cramps</strong> - due to thiazide diuretics, lead.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="the-endocrine-system">The Endocrine System<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-endocrine-system" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The endocrine system may also be affected, which usually manifests as decreased libido. This is often a result of lead, mercury or other heavy metals which act as sympathetic blocking agents.</p>
<p>As you will have seen, one poison causes many changes in different body systems. The next topic, the management of poisoning looks at measures aimed at preventing/minimising these effects, while providing supportive measures to keep systems working, thereby giving the body a chance of recovery.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="management-of-poisoning">Management of Poisoning<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#management-of-poisoning" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="general-plan-for-the-management-of-acute-poisoning">General Plan for the Management of Acute Poisoning<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#general-plan-for-the-management-of-acute-poisoning" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The following steps should be implemented depending on the patient's condition:</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Ensure that the airway, ventilation and blood pressure are adequate</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Assess the level of consciousness</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Obtain information about the poison if there is uncertainty about its toxicity or appropriate treatment</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Consider whether an antidote is available, appropriate or necessary</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Consider the need for measures to prevent the absorption of the poison</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Consider whether an emergency analysis should be requested</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Institute a programme of continuing care</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Consider whether it is possible or desirable to attempt to increase the elimination of the poison</p>
</li>
</ul>
<p>In acute poisoning, the first priority is to ensure that the airway is patent and that alveolar ventilation and circulation are adequate to maintain life, while decisions are made about further treatment.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="maintaining-the-patent-airway">Maintaining the Patent Airway<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#maintaining-the-patent-airway" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Obstruction of the upper airway is one of the main causes of death in patients dying from poisoning outside hospital. Maintaining a clear airway for the patient automatically improves alveolar ventilation and often restores blood pressure if breathing has not stopped. It is important to do the following:</p>
<ul>
<li>Remove dental plates if the patient has them and keep aside for labelling and storing until the patient is conscious</li>
<li>Pull the tongue forward</li>
<li>Remove saliva or vomitus from the mouth and pharynx by use of a suction catheter</li>
<li>In an emergency a swab or handkerchief wrapped round a finger is also effective</li>
</ul>
<p>If the patient is deeply unconscious, insert an oral airway device or nasal airway device or intubate the patient.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="ventilation">Ventilation<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#ventilation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Establishing a patent airway improves ventilation. However, if the rate or depth of respiration is inadequate, do not wait for the result of an arterial blood gas analysis. Instead, use an ambu bag and administer oxygen. In less urgent circumstances, the adequacy of ventilation is best assessed by arterial blood gas analysis.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="blood-pressure">Blood Pressure<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#blood-pressure" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>In hypotension, the minimum systolic blood pressure reading is <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mn>80</mn><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">H</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">g</mi></mrow></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">80\mathrm{mmHg}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.8778em; vertical-align: -0.1944em;"></span><span class="mord">80</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm" style="margin-right: 0.01389em;">mmHg</span></span></span></span></span></span> in young adults and <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mn>90</mn><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">H</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">g</mi></mrow></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">90\mathrm{mmHg}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.8778em; vertical-align: -0.1944em;"></span><span class="mord">90</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm" style="margin-right: 0.01389em;">mmHg</span></span></span></span></span></span> in those above 40 years. However, these values are arbitrary and more reliance should be placed on organ perfusion as assessed by the patient's mental state (if conscious), skin temperature or hourly urine output.</p>
<p>When hypotension is a problem:</p>
<ol>
<li>Clear the airway, improve ventilation and administer oxygen as this will abolish hypotension.</li>
<li>Elevate the lower limbs to increase the venous return to the heart.</li>
<li>In those who fail to respond to steps one and two, administer intravascular volume expanders intravenously, for example, plasma or dextran.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="assessment-of-level-of-consciousness">Assessment of Level of Consciousness<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#assessment-of-level-of-consciousness" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The Edinburgh method of assessment is recommended. This is based on the patient's response to commands and pain. The following grading scheme is applied:</p>
<ul>
<li>Grade 0 the patient is fully conscious.</li>
<li>Grade 1 the patient is drowsy but obeys commands.</li>
<li>Grade 2 is unresponsive to commands but responds well to pain.</li>
<li>Grade 3 the patient is unresponsive to commands and exhibits minimal response to pain.</li>
<li>Grade 4 the patient is completely unresponsive.</li>
</ul>
<p>By definition, patients in Grades 2 - 4 are unconscious.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="identifying-the-type-of-poison">Identifying the Type of Poison<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#identifying-the-type-of-poison" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>In order to ensure that you are able to identify the poison, you should ensure that:</p>
<ul>
<li>You have no doubt about the correct spelling of the poison.</li>
<li>Give the brand name of the drug or commercial product whenever possible.</li>
<li>If you do not know the precise name of the poison, state its purpose, for example, dry cleaning agent.</li>
<li>Do not be dismayed if information about poison is sketchy or not available. The lack of information simply reflects the rarity of poisoning by the substance.</li>
<li>Do not expect poison information services to identify plants and mushrooms from descriptions given over the telephone. This must be identified by a sample.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="antidotes">Antidotes<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#antidotes" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The administration of antidotes to certain poisons can occasionally produce dramatic and life saving improvement in a patient's condition.</p>
<p>However, contrary to popular belief, antidotes are available for a very small number of poisons, most of which are uncommon in everyday clinical practice.</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Poison</th><th>Antidotes</th><th>Mode of Action</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Anticholinergic compounds</td><td>Neostigmine salicylates</td><td>Cholinesterase inhibitor</td></tr><tr><td>Anticoagulant</td><td>Vitamin K, fresh frozen plasma clotting factors</td><td>Pharmacological antagonist - replace missing clotting factors</td></tr><tr><td>Adrenergic blockers</td><td>Isoprenaline</td><td>Pharmacological antagonist - stimulates myocardial adenocyclase</td></tr><tr><td>Cyanide ethylene glycol</td><td>Dicobalt edotate Ethanol</td><td>Chelating agents - competitive substrate for alcohol</td></tr><tr><td>Heavy metals</td><td>Demercapol penicillamine</td><td>Dehydrogenase celating agents</td></tr><tr><td>Iron salts</td><td>Desferoximine</td><td>Chelating agent</td></tr><tr><td>Methanol</td><td>Ethanol</td><td>Competitive substance for alcohol dehydrogenase</td></tr><tr><td>Narcotic analgesics</td><td>Naloxone</td><td>Charmacological antagonist</td></tr><tr><td>Organophosphate</td><td>Atropine, Palidoxine,</td><td>Acetylcholine antagonist</td></tr><tr><td>Paracetamol</td><td>N-acetylcysteine methionine cysteamine</td><td>PSH donors or glutathione</td></tr><tr><td>Pentazoline</td><td>Naloxone</td><td>Pharmacological antagonist</td></tr><tr><td>Sympathomietics</td><td>B-adrenergic blockers</td><td>Pharmacological antagonist</td></tr><tr><td>Thallium</td><td>Prussian blue</td><td>Chelating agent</td></tr></tbody></table>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="respiratory-failure">Respiratory Failure<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#respiratory-failure" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Respiration refers to a process by which oxygen is taken in through the airway to the alveoli, diffuses into the blood, is transported through the red blood cell, released and utilised by the</p>
<p>Bishops Computers
Bishops Computers</p>
<p>tissues and the waste products carried away from the cells and excreted.</p>
<p>Respiratory failure occurs when the above chain of events are broken. It is broadly defined as the inability of the body to maintain acceptable arterial values of oxygen, carbon dioxide and the blood pH. Respiratory failure may be due to a single factor or many. It may be caused by:</p>
<ul>
<li>The inability of air to enter the airway due to obstruction, whatever the cause.</li>
<li>Poor respiratory muscle tone. Examples include congenital muscle atrophy, Guillen Barre syndrome, tetanus etc.</li>
<li>Impaired gaseous exchange at the alveolar capillary junction like in pulmonary oedema.</li>
<li>The inability of the blood to transport oxygen and waste products of respiration.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="management-of-respiratory-failure">Management of Respiratory Failure<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#management-of-respiratory-failure" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The first step in treating any patient with airway obstruction is to open it. This is always done by the head-tilt, chin-lift or jaw thrush method initially. It may then be desirable to use an artificial airway to keep the victim's airway unobstructed. An artificial airway is particularly useful if the victim is breathing spontaneously or if rescue breathing is applied by a bag mask device rather than mouth-to-mouth or mouth-to-nose.</p>
<p>Two types of basic artificial airway devices commonly used in critical care settings are the oro-pharyngeal airway and the naso-pharyngeal airway.</p>
<p>As the name implies, the oro-pharyngeal airway extends from the mouth (oro) into the back of the throat (pharyngeal). It is a curved plastic or hard rubber device designed to fit over the back of the tongue and hence hold the tongue away from the posterior part of the throat.</p>
<p>The oro-pharyngeal airway is useful in deeply unconscious patients who are breathing spontaneously or who are being ventilated by mask. It should not be used on conscious or semi-conscious patients because their reflexes are still intact. The presence of the oro-pharyngeal airway against the back of the throat causes gagging and sometimes vomiting and laryngospasms.</p>
<p>Oro-pharyngeal airway devices come in different sizes, that is, infant, child and adult. You need to choose the one that conforms best to the patient's dimensions. Hold the airway against the patient's face. If it is the right size, it will extend from the mouth to the angle of the jaw.</p>
<p>The naso-pharyngeal airway is a soft rubber tube that is inserted through the nose (naso) and extends down into the back of the pharynx, behind the tongue thereby allowing free passage of air from the nose to the lower airway. This airway is better tolerated by a semi-conscious patient than is the oro-pharyngeal airway.</p>
<p>The naso-pharyngeal airway is useful in situations where:</p>
<ul>
<li>The patient's mouth cannot be opened</li>
<li>There is trauma in the patient's mouth or the lower jaw</li>
<li>The patient will not tolerate oro-pharyngeal airway but is not sufficiently conscious to maintain an open airway by themselves</li>
</ul>
<p>The naso-pharyngeal airway comes in only one size and it can only be used by adults.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="remember">Remember:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#remember" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>The naso-pharyngeal airway is better tolerated than the oro-pharyngeal airway in the semi-conscious patient.</li>
<li>Do not use the naso-pharyngeal when there is trauma to the nose or suspected skull fracture.</li>
<li>Lubricate the naso-pharyngeal airway well before inserting.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="advanced-airway-control">Advanced Airway Control<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#advanced-airway-control" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Endo-tracheal intubation provides the most definitive control over the patient's airway.</p>
<p>The curved endo-tracheal tube also seals off the airway from foreign material. When placed within the trachea it is possible to give 100% oxygen without the danger of causing gastric distension or aspiration.</p>
<p>Endo-tracheal intubation requires skills and constant practice.</p>
<p>Bishops Computers
Bishops Computers</p>
<p>Bishops Computers
Page 364 of 425</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="rigid-laryngoscope">Rigid Laryngoscope<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#rigid-laryngoscope" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260111-caf403f9.jpeg" alt="img-2.jpeg"></p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260111-9b975e7c.jpeg" alt="img-3.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="naso-pharyngeal-airway-and-shortened-endotracheal-tube">Naso-pharyngeal Airway and Shortened Endotracheal Tube<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#naso-pharyngeal-airway-and-shortened-endotracheal-tube" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="murphy-endo-tracheal-tube">Murphy Endo-tracheal Tube<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#murphy-endo-tracheal-tube" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260111-c5404fe5.jpeg" alt="img-4.jpeg"></p>
<p>The following equipment is required for endotracheal intubation:</p>
<ul>
<li>Two to three endo-tracheal tubes of different sizes</li>
<li>20ml syringes</li>
<li>Itemostal</li>
<li>Bandages</li>
<li>Scissors</li>
<li>Plastic connection for endo-tracheal tubes</li>
<li>Malleable stylet</li>
<li>Water soluble lubricating jell</li>
<li>Laryngoscope handle</li>
<li>One to two curved (Macintosh) blades</li>
<li>One to two straight (Miller) blades</li>
<li>Spare batteries for laryngoscope</li>
<li>Magill forceps</li>
<li>Guition unit</li>
<li>Regid (tonsil tip)</li>
<li>Guition catheter</li>
<li>Several flexible suture catheters (Sterile)</li>
<li>Bite unlock or oro-pharyngeal airway</li>
<li>Adhesive tapes or umbilical tape (preferred)</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="mechanical-ventilation-support">Mechanical Ventilation Support<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#mechanical-ventilation-support" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Mechanical ventilation support refers to provision of breathing, when one is not able to do so spontaneously on their own. Ventilation may be achieved through use of exhaled air as in mouth-to-mouth or nose, or giving air/oxygen either manually or mechanically using a ventilator.</p>
<p>Mechanical ventilation is used in an ICU set-up because it can support breathing for a long period of time and can be manipulated to suit the patient needs.</p>
<p>Though mechanical ventilation is excellent in maintaining ventilation, it must be avoided when unnecessary because it is not a cure in itself but a temporary measure and must be initiated early when indicated for good outcome.</p>
<p>The rationale for using mechanical ventilation on a patient include:</p>
<ul>
<li>To provide the pulmonary system with mechanical power to maintain physiological ventilation.</li>
<li>Manipulate the ventilatory pattern and airway pressures for the purpose of improving the efficiency of respiration.</li>
<li>To decrease myocardial work by decreasing work of breathing and improving efficiency.</li>
</ul>
<p>Bishops Computers
Page 365 of 425
Bishops Computers</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="mode-of-ventilation">Mode of Ventilation<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#mode-of-ventilation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>When your patient is not able to maintain breathing and is being supported by a ventilator, it's important to have an idea of the mode that the patient is using.</p>
<p>A mode is a mechanism that initiates inspiration in mechanical ventilation. In normal body function, the respiratory centre responds to increased carbon dioxide levels in the blood and initiates an inspiration. Likewise the ventilator has settings that initiates and maintains breathing.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="controlled-mode">Controlled Mode<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#controlled-mode" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The ventilator is set to deliver a specific number of breaths per minute. It therefore ventilates the patient at a preset frequency. This mode is used when the patient has no respiratory effort.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="assisted-ventilation">Assisted Ventilation<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#assisted-ventilation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>This is used when the patient is making inadequate number of breaths. The ventilator only supplements the patient's effort. There are two types of assisted modes. It can either be mandatory or synchronised with the patient's respiratory function.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="other-airway-manipulation">Other Airway Manipulation<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#other-airway-manipulation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>For example with Positive End Expiratory Pressure (PEEP) or Continuous Positive Airway Pressure (CPAP) reduces the intrapulmonary shunting and increases the arterial oxygen tension.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="cardiac-arrest">Cardiac Arrest<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#cardiac-arrest" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Cardiac arrest results in death, which occurs when the heart stops pumping blood. This may be expected in old age or as a result of certain illnesses. It may also occur suddenly and unexpectedly in younger people as a result of electric shock, drowning, anaphylactic reactions, trauma or heart attack.</p>
<p>A person is judged to be clinically dead when they stop breathing, when there is no cardiac activity and the usual signs of life are absent. Feeling the carotid pulse is the most definite way of checking for effective cardiac activity.</p>
<p>There are three general types of fatal cardiac arrhythmias:</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Ventricular standstill or a systole (no heartbeat).</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Ventricular fibrillation, where the heart muscles are quivering, which indicates no contraction and no blood flow.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Pulseless electrical activity, which has no perceptible pulse or blood pressure.</p>
</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="how-can-you-know-if-a-person-is-in-a-state-of-cardiac-arrest">How can you know if a person is in a state of cardiac arrest?<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#how-can-you-know-if-a-person-is-in-a-state-of-cardiac-arrest" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The following four diagnostic signs would indicate that cardiac arrest has occurred:</p>
<ul>
<li>Breathing stops</li>
<li>No palpable pulse</li>
<li>No heartbeat</li>
<li>Pupils fixed and dilated</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="management-of-a-patient-with-cardiac-arrest">Management of a Patient with Cardiac Arrest<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#management-of-a-patient-with-cardiac-arrest" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Emergency artificial circulation is restored by intermittent external chest compressions. In adults, a rate of about 80 compressions per minute is recommended. However, this cardiac compression must be accompanied by artificial mechanical lungs inflation at a rate of 20 breaths per minute. This means the ratio of 15:2 compressions to ventilations should be used during CPR.</p>
<p>In children and neonates, ratio of 5:1, cardiac compression to ventilation is used. If this does not achieve the required result, Advanced Cardiac Life Support (ACLS) has to be applied. The present ACLS protocol is based on four components:</p>
<ul>
<li>Early assessment of the victim</li>
<li>Early CPR</li>
<li>Early defibrillation</li>
<li>Early access to ACLS</li>
</ul>
<p>As you have just learnt, the present ACLS protocol is based on four components.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="early-assessment-of-the-victim">Early Assessment of the Victim<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#early-assessment-of-the-victim" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>This refers to identifying the need for resuscitation and the activation of emergency response system like shouting for help.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="early-cpr">Early CPR<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#early-cpr" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Chest compressions and ventilations are started early in order to get better results. Early CPR determines the quality of resuscitation. Establish locations for chest compressions. As you can see from the illustration opposite, this should be two fingers above xiphisternum in adults.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="early-defibrillation">Early Defibrillation<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#early-defibrillation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>In defibrillation, electrodes/pads are placed on the patient's chest and a measured electrical energy is passed through the body to create external electrical counter shocks that may restart the heartbeat. 200, 300, 360 or 400</p>
<p>joules may be used to defibrillate ventricular fibrillation. The outcome depends on how early this arrhythmia is identified and defibrillated.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="early-access-to-acls">Early Access to ACLS<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#early-access-to-acls" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The nurse must be able to identify victims requiring advanced cardiac life support promptly. Advanced methods of restoring/maintaining patient's life like intubation, cardiac arrhythmia monitoring, use of drugs etc. are taken.</p>
<p>Epinephrine (adrenaline), amiaderone, lignocain, deltezan atropine and vassopressin have been accepted as the drug of choice in ACLS protocols among other drugs.</p>
<p>To conclude, cardiac arrest is managed by primarily making attempts to restart the heartbeat mechanically while at the same time maintaining lung ventilation.</p>
<p>Advanced methods of cardiac management may be used. Cardio version or pace maker therapy, and sometimes open cardiac massage/defibrillation may be used.</p>
<p>Now move on from cardiac arrest to focus on the management of multiple injuries.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="multiple-injury">Multiple Injury<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#multiple-injury" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The following are general principles and concepts in the management of multiple injuries:</p>
<ul>
<li>The presence of signs of hypotension requires immediate resuscitation and surgical exploration.</li>
<li>When the Mean Arterial Pressure (MAP) fails to respond to intravenous fluid volume of &gt;5 litres, urgent surgical exploration is mandatory to control the bleeding. Fluid resuscitation is continued during surgery.</li>
<li>Cardiac tamponade must be considered in stab wounds and gunshot wounds of the chest and upper abdomen. Elevated Central Venous Pressure (CVP) is usually the first sign of tamponade.</li>
<li>Computed Topographic Scans (CTS) of the head, chest and abdomen may be done to elicit extent of damage.</li>
<li>Repeated physical examination provides important information that helps in diagnosis and management.</li>
<li>Suspected fracture of the spine has to be immobilised before attempting to move the victim, as failure to do so may lead to irreversible damage to neurofunctions.</li>
</ul>
<p>In conclusion, the choice of management measures will be guided by ensuring an accurate diagnosis of the extent of body damage. After this, corrective and life sustaining measures are to be taken to allow the body time to recover.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="acute-chronic-and-end-stage-renal-failure">Acute, Chronic and End Stage Renal Failure<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#acute-chronic-and-end-stage-renal-failure" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Acute renal failure describes an abrupt and almost complete cessation of renal function. Chronic renal failure, on the other hand, describes an irreversible reduction in renal functions of a progressive nature. While acute renal failure may be reversed and the patient restored back to normal health, chronic renal failure and end stage renal disease are only treatable by either dialysis or renal transplant.</p>
<p>There are several identifiable causes of renal failure. These include:</p>
<ul>
<li>Glomerulopathy, especially necrotising proliferative and membranoproliferative. The latter is rapidly progressive due to streptococcal and urinal infections, lupus erythematosus, eclampsia and mixed cryoglobulinaemia.</li>
<li>Vascular and thrombotic disease, for example, malignant hypertension, scherodema, hypersensitivity, angiitis, etc.</li>
<li>Interstitial disease, for example, allergic, papillary necrosis.</li>
<li>Functional (pre) renal failure such as severe volume depletion, shock, trauma and/or heart failure.</li>
<li>Acute renal failure/parenchyma failure (acute tubular necrosis, vasomotor nephropathy).</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="acute-renal-failure">Acute Renal Failure<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#acute-renal-failure" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Acute renal failure can present itself in several ways. These are some of the signs to look for in a patient:</p>
<ul>
<li>Abrupt decrease in the Glomerular Filtration Rate (GFR)</li>
<li>Oliguria (urine outlet below 400ml/day (Swan et al 1953)</li>
<li>As a result of low level glomerular filtration rate, the serum creatinine concentrate rises steadily by 1 to 4 mg dl per day</li>
<li>Total anuria (cessation of urine production) is common in uncomplicated acute renal failure due to urinary outflow obstruction.</li>
</ul>
<p>Bishops Computers
Bishops Computers</p>
<p>Bishops Computers
Page 367 of 425
Bishops Computers</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="prevention-and-management">Prevention and Management<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#prevention-and-management" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>When a patient is at risk of developing Acute Renal Failure (ARF) because of disordered haemodynamic disorder, rather than nephrotoxins, you can manage it by prompt and full restoration of blood volume and adequate cardiovascular tools. This is achieved by administering fluids in conditions characterised by severe decrease in blood volume, for example, severe burns, haemorrhages, and dehydration.</p>
<p>While the use of diuretics, for example, furosemide (lasix) increases urine volume it has no effect on clinical outcome. When the underlying cause is not addressed, the side effects of furosemide, such as permanent deafness and volume depletion or hypokalaemia should be weighed against the somewhat minimal benefits of their use.</p>
<p>Management should be directed at preventing complications. This should include meticulous attention to fluid and electrolyte balance, drug use and nutritional support.</p>
<p>Unfortunately, patients with sepsis, multi-organ failure, or trauma, often have life threatening complications such as hyperkalemia, acidosis or volume overload. In such cases, dialysis therapy should be commenced early and aggressively to prevent complications. Intermittent haemodialysis is the most widely used.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="chronic-renal-failure-and-end-stage-renal-disease">Chronic Renal Failure and End Stage Renal Disease<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#chronic-renal-failure-and-end-stage-renal-disease" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Chronic Renal Failure (CRF) and End Stage Renal Disease are functional diagnosis caused by a number of diseases and present themselves with a progressive and generally irreversible decline in Glomerular Filtration Rate (GFR). At the initial phase of advancing renal failure, the patient is unaware of the problem so does not seek help until it presents with uremic syndrome when most organs are no longer functioning normally. End stage renal disease is present when the nephrones are destroyed and cannot make urine. The patient is therefore having renal failure.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="management">Management<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#management" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The first management consideration is the preservation of renal function. To this end, the maintenance of extra-cellular volume status is of primary importance to ensure adequate renal perfusion. However, because of the kidney's reduced ability to excrete salt and water, care must be exercised in administering fluid to the patient.</p>
<p>In chronic renal failure the patient is able to make little urine, while in end stage renal disease most of the nephrones are completely destroyed.</p>
<p>Hypertension, which is common in both renal failures, should be treated with anti-hypertensive, such as sodium nitroprusside, which is often used because it has the advantage of rapid action at the onset with a short half-life. However, its metabolic by-products, that is, cyanide and thiocyanate, have long half-lives and accumulate in renal insufficiency. Toxicity may occur within 48 hours, signs of which will include metabolic acidosis, confusion, hyperreflexia and seizures. Because of this risk, other alternatives are used which include methyldopa, hydrazine, esmolo, etc.</p>
<p>Radio-contrast agents must be avoided. Since drugs or metabolites often accumulate in renal failure, the administration of drugs should be avoided as much as possible.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="section-4-special-procedures-and-investigations">SECTION 4: SPECIAL PROCEDURES AND INVESTIGATIONS<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#section-4-special-procedures-and-investigations" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="introduction">Introduction<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#introduction" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>In this section you will look at special investigations carried out in critical care patients to include blood analysis, urinalysis and radiological examination. The procedures relating to dialysis, central venous pressure and tracheotomy will also be covered.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="objectives">Objectives<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#objectives" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>By the end of this section you will be able to:</p>
<ul>
<li>List the rationale for carrying out blood and urine analysis in a critically ill patient</li>
<li>Describe the investigations performed on the blood and urine of a critically ill patient</li>
<li>Describe the specific radiological examinations performed on a critically ill patient</li>
<li>Describe dialysis, central venous pressure monitoring, and tracheotomy</li>
<li>Describe the management of patients during and after dialysis and tracheotomy</li>
</ul>
<p>Bishops Computers

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="rationale-for-blood-and-urine-analysis">Rationale for Blood and Urine Analysis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#rationale-for-blood-and-urine-analysis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Blood analysis in a critically ill patient is performed for the following reasons:</p>
<ul>
<li>To establish the cause of coma/unconsciousness that could not otherwise be explained.</li>
<li>To establish the values of various constituents in blood so as to compare with the normal values, for example, the normal potassium value range is 3.4 - 5.0 ml/litre. A blood value of below 3 mmol/litre indicates hypokalaemia.</li>
<li>To monitor patient improvement following therapy, for instance, after administering insulin in the management of diabetes mellitus. Blood sugar testing is done to monitor the effectiveness of therapy. The blood sugar is expected to fall after insulin therapy.</li>
<li>To compare the various constituent values with normal values in the process of reaching a differential diagnosis, for example, raised blood sugar above the normal value may be the only indicator of latent diabetes.</li>
<li>To establish alkalosis or acidosis and take corrective measures before complication sets in.</li>
<li>To establish facts that will be used to determine the management of the patient so as to bring the values back to normal.</li>
</ul>
<p>When analysing urine, the main aim is to detect abnormalities like protenuria and glucosuria, which may indicate endocrine failure and other metabolic problems in the patient.</p>
<p>You will now focus on investigations that should be carried out on the blood and urine.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="blood-analysis">Blood Analysis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#blood-analysis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>There are several types of blood analysis.</p>
<ul>
<li>Blood for urea and electrolytes</li>
</ul>
<table><thead><tr><th>Normal</th><th>values:</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Potassium 3.4 - 5.0 mmol/L</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Calcium 2.12 - 2.6 mmol/L</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Magnesium 0.7 - 1.0 mmol/L</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Sodium 135 - 143 mmol/L</td><td></td></tr></tbody></table>

<ul>

Male 4.5 - 6.5 millions/mm3

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="blood-gas-analysis">Blood Gas Analysis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#blood-gas-analysis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>This gives information about carbon dioxide excretion, oxygenation and acid base balance of the body. The state of these three indicates the metabolic condition of the body.</p>
<p>The normal values of blood gas pressure to be expected are as follows:</p>
<ul>
<li>Partial pressure of oxygen pa02 = 11 - 13kpa (83 - 98mmhg)</li>
<li>Partial pressure of carbon dioxide paCO = 4.8 - 6.0kpa (36 - 45mmhg)</li>
<li>Blood pH 7.36 - 7.44</li>
<li>Haemoglobin oxygen saturation  95%</li>
<li>Bicarbonate levels 22 - 26 mmol/L</li>
<li>Base excess -3 to +3.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="urine-analysis">Urine Analysis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#urine-analysis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The main investigations here include total urinalysis, culture and sensitivity.</p>
<p>The graphic opposite indicates the normal constituent values of urine.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260111-32f1ed82.jpeg" alt="img-5.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="radiological-examinations">Radiological Examinations<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#radiological-examinations" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>This is an x-ray examination of a body part or organ. It can be a plain x-ray or special, which requires the introduction of a contrast media - a substance that will allow a structure to be outlined clearly in an x-ray image.</p>
<p>Plain x-rays are useful in suspected fractures of bones and in chest diseases/conditions, because they can help to confirm the suspected diagnosis. Special x-rays such as bronchography (x-ray of the bronchous after instillation of radiopaque substance), pulmonary angiography (x-ray taken after injecting contrast media into pulmonary artery) etc, outline the cavities so that their structures can be seen clearly in the x-ray.</p>
<p>Others such as tomography, magnetic resonance imaging and ultrasonography, will illustrate tissues in order of their differences in mass (density), so that it is possible to distinguish tumours from normal organs as tumour cells have different density from normal structures.</p>
<p>Your role is to take into consideration the psychological and physical care of the patient before, during and after the x-rays. For example, in a female patient who is scheduled for an x-ray, it is necessary to inquire about the possibility of pregnancy. Unless absolutely necessary, an x-ray should not be performed on a pregnant woman because the x-ray has adverse effect on the growing foetus.</p>
<p>Magnetic Resonance Imaging (MRI) is contraindicated for patients with pacemakers or</p>
<p>implanted metallic devices due to the effect of magnetic energy on the metals. The patient needs to be well informed of the type of x-ray as well as their expected role in the pre-procedure preparation, intra-procedure expectation and post-procedure care.</p>
<p>You should be on the look out for reaction to contrast media used while at the same time observing the general condition of the patient during and after the procedure.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="dialysis">Dialysis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#dialysis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Dialysis is the process of removing excess fluids, urea and other wastes from the body artificially. Dialysis is indicated in those patients with:</p>
<ul>
<li>Rising creatinine levels of 100 micrommo/litre in 24 hours.</li>
<li>Creatinine clearance of below 0.1 - 0.15mls/min/body kilogram weight.</li>
<li>Hyperkalaemia greater than 7mmol/L with signs of intravascular and extra vascular fluid overload.</li>
<li>Symptoms of uraemia in a patient with acute renal failure such as pericarditis (inflammation of membrane covering the heart), gastric intestinal bleeding and mental changes.</li>
<li>Acute poisoning with mental changes.</li>
</ul>
<p>There are two methods of dialysis; peritoneal dialysis and haemodialysis. In peritoneal dialysis contraindication includes abdominal sepsis or abdominal surgery less than three days previously. In haemodialysis, contraindications include increased blood coagulation, haemodynamic instability and lack of circulation.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="peritoneal-dialysis-pd">Peritoneal Dialysis (PD)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#peritoneal-dialysis-pd" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>This involves the introduction of sterile dialysing fluid (dialysate) through a catheter into the abdominal peritoneal cavity.</p>
<p>Through the process of diffusion, osmosis and active transport, excess fluid and solutes move from intravascular (blood) vessels, through the semi-permeable peritorial membrane, into the dialysing fluid. The fluid is then drained from the abdominal cavity by gravity.</p>
<p>This process is repeated over a number of cycles. Urden and Stacy (2000) argue that the introduction of 3.5 litres of dialysate per hour will clear the urea at the rate of  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mn>26</mn><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">l</mi></mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">/</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">26\mathrm{ml}/</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 1em; vertical-align: -0.25em;"></span><span class="mord">26</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm">ml</span></span><span class="mord">/</span></span></span></span></span>  minute during acute phase of renal failure.</p>
<p>Bishops Computers</p>
<p>Bishops Computers</p>
<p>As a nurse, during PD, you must ensure that the dialysate is at the body temperature (37 degrees Celsius) before introduction. This ensures the patient's comfort and provides vasodilation of blood vessels hence increasing solute transport in the peritoneum.</p>
<p>The fluid should not be left in the peritoneal cavity longer than required and the amount introduced must be measured so as to compare with the amount drained during each cycle. This helps to assess if any of the fluid is being retained. Ideally, the amount drained should be more than that introduced due to the added waste (for example, urea, excess intravascular and extra-vascular fluid) being removed.</p>
<p>You must also monitor urine output in acute renal failure. This is expected to increase with the effect of dialysis. The drainage bag for the dialysate should always be at a lower level than the patient's bed because it flows by gravity. It has been noted that sitting the patient at 45 or more, if conscious, will assist the outflow (Gutch et al:1993).</p>
<p>After the expected cycles are over, remove the catheter by cutting the sutures, pull the catheter out and dress the wound under aseptic technique.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260111-68fe17a0.jpeg" alt="img-6.jpeg">
Dialysate infused into peritoneal cavity</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260111-949b4261.jpeg" alt="img-7.jpeg">
Dialysate draining out</p>
<p>A patient receiving two-way peritoneal dialysis</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260111-aecbfbb9.jpeg" alt="img-8.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="haemodialysis">Haemodialysis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#haemodialysis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Haemodialysis has been defined as a technique of separating/removing particles/fluids from the blood by differences in their ability to pass through a semi-permeable membrane. It is therefore a process of purifying blood. In other words, this implies the separation and removal of electrolytes, fluids and toxins mechanically.</p>
<p>The equipment used is referred to as a haemodialysis machine. Blood is circulated outside the body through synthetic tubing to a dialyser. The components of a haemodialysis are shown in the graphic opposite.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260111-abe1660c.jpeg" alt="img-9.jpeg"></p>
<p>As blood flows through the haemodialysis, the dialysis, by the process of ultra filtration osmosis and diffusion, removes excess fluid, electrolytes</p>
<p>Bishops Computers</p>
<p>Bishops Computers</p>
<p>and toxins from the blood. Your role as a nurse during haemodialysis is to:</p>
<ul>
<li>Prevent infection</li>
<li>Observe vital signs for the patient and the machine</li>
<li>Monitor for changes in arterial or venous pressure</li>
<li>Ensure the patient lies in comfortable position</li>
<li>Ensure adherence to aseptic technique throughout</li>
<li>Provide comfort by reassuring the patient and relatives</li>
<li>Teach/counsel on renal diet and alternative therapy like transplant</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="central-venous-pressure-cvp-measurement">Central Venous Pressure (CVP) Measurement<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#central-venous-pressure-cvp-measurement" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>You may have during your practice in the nursing career been involved in taking a patient's blood pressure as a basic routine monitoring of the patient's vital signs (pulse, blood pressure, respiration, temperature). You will now extend this to central venous pressure monitoring. The word pressure refers to a force being exerted in a place.</p>
<p>CVP, therefore, implies the force being exerted by the circulating blood volume in a large central vein, for example, the jugular, sub-clavian and femoral veins. This force is measured in millimetre of mercury or centimetre of water using a manometer.</p>
<p>CVP monitoring is indicated in a critically ill patient who has significant alterations in fluid volume. It is used as a guide in the effectiveness of fluid replacement therapy in hypovolaemic. It is also used to monitor the effectiveness of diuresis in a patient suffering from fluid overload and retention that is on diuretic therapy (Stacy: 2000).</p>
<p>The CVP is measured by inserting a catheter into the internal angular veins, subclavian, vena cava or right atrium. You should connect a saline manometer between the cannula and drip set.</p>
<p>The pressure should always be measured with the patient in the same position. You should ensure that the patient is lying flat each time as posture has an effect on the reading. The following steps should be followed when undertaking this procedure:</p>
<ol>
<li>Ensure that the drip runs freely.</li>
<li>Set the zero position of the scale of the manometer at the inter-space between the sternum and the back, that is, at the level of the right atrium of the heart.</li>
<li>Open the drip while the clip connecting the manometer to the patient is closed so that the manometer fills up with saline, then close the drip.</li>
<li>Turn the clip connecting the manometer to the line going to the patient.</li>
</ol>
<p>The saline in the manometer should fall until it stabilises. The reading on the scale at this level indicates the CVP. The reading is in terms of centimetres of saline. If it is above the zero point, that means the pressure is positive, if it is below the zero point, then the pressure is negative. The saline in the manometer should swing with each respiration. The normal CVP is 3 - 8cm of water or 2 - 5mm of mercury (Stacy: 2000).</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="how-do-you-interpret-cvp-monitoring-findings">How do you interpret CVP monitoring findings?<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#how-do-you-interpret-cvp-monitoring-findings" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>A low CVP usually occurs in the hypovolemic patient. To compensate for normal cardiac output, the heart rate increase. This increase produces the tachycardia observed in a patient in hypovolemic shock. An elevated CVP above normal occurs in cases of fluid overload. This causes the heart to increase its contractile force in an attempt to move the fluid volume in the intravascular space, hence raising the blood pressure.</p>
<p>As a critical care nurse, you should monitor the CVP trends to determine subsequent interventions for fluid volume management in the patient.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="tracheotomy">Tracheotomy<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#tracheotomy" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>A tracheotomy is an artificial opening in the trachea that provides access to the airway below the larynx.</p>
<p>Tracheotomy is mainly indicated for an obstruction of the upper airways as may occur in patients with bulbar palsy, prolonged retention of bronchial secretions or carcinoma of the larynx.</p>
<p>The graphic opposite illustrates the method of performing a tracheotomy on a critically ill patient.</p>
<p>Bishops Computers
Bishops Computers</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260111-7817c06b.jpeg" alt="img-10.jpeg"></p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260111-5f3b20f2.jpeg" alt="img-11.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="management-of-a-patient-with-a-tracheotomy-tube">Management of a Patient with a Tracheotomy Tube<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#management-of-a-patient-with-a-tracheotomy-tube" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Since the tracheotomy tube bypasses the humidification process of the air, which normally occurs in the nasopharynx, you should artificially humidify the air passing through the tube by a condenser humidifier such as humidivent (portex) or first place a moist gauze swab over the tracheotomy.</p>
<p>You should ensure regular suction of the tube to remove secretion. The patient requires chest physiotherapy by an experienced physiotherapist during the period the tube is in place, especially if they are unconscious. You should always have a tracheotomy tray containing curved artery forceps to be used in an emergency to be inserted in the tracheotomy site should the tube come out by accident. Other items needed in the tray are sterile suction catheters and spare sterile tracheotomy tubes of different sizes.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="management-of-a-patient-with-a-tracheotomy-tube">Management of a Patient with a Tracheotomy Tube<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#management-of-a-patient-with-a-tracheotomy-tube" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The tracheotomy tube can be changed if it becomes blocked. Change the tubes as follows:</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Inform the patient that you intend to change the tube</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Put on sterile gloves</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Ask assistant to cut the tapes of the tube already in place</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Suction the patient</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Remove the tube with the left hand and insert the new one with your right hand, curving it in and down the trachea</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Secure the new tube in position</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Ask the patient to cough if they are conscious</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Reassure the patient</p>
</li>
</ul>
<p>If the tracheotomy was performed as a temporary emergency measure, it is usually left in use for five to seven days. Before removing, check that the patient can breathe normally by occluding the tube with a sterile gloved palm of the hand. The tracheotomy site is then dressed. Any skin sutures can be removed after seven days.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="rehabilitation-and-counselling">Rehabilitation and Counselling<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#rehabilitation-and-counselling" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Although counselling will be covered as a subject in module four, the role of rehabilitation and counselling in critical care nursing will be briefly covered here.</p>
<p>You may agree that if a patient is either unconscious, on ventilation or dialysis machine, relatives experience some concern. The patient is also likely to have problems coping and adjusting after gaining full recovery. At the same time, the patient may need to be weaned from the machines. All this calls for rehabilitation and counselling of both the relatives and the patient. To achieve this, you should be able to:</p>
<ol>
<li>Have an open, honest discussion of the expected outcome of each patient with the patient and relatives throughout the period of therapy.</li>
<li>Advise each patient and relative on alternatives available in their situation, for example, change of occupation.</li>
<li>Inform them of others who have coped in similar situation.</li>
<li>Refer the patient to relevant rehabilitation and counselling services available.</li>
<li>Continue giving support through the established health care system during the post-therapy period.</li>
</ol>
<p>Bishops Computers
Bishops Computers</p>
<p>UNIT FIVE PART TWO: THEATRE NURSING</p>
<p>This unit focuses on issues such as the historical background of theatre nursing, legal aspects in theatre and the general layout of the operating theatre. It also covers the preparation of the operating room, the equipment and the roles and functions of the nurses.</p>
<p>This unit is composed of three sections:</p>
<p>Section One: Introduction to the Operating Theatre.</p>
<p>Section Two: Safety and Infection Prevention in Theatre.</p>
<p>Section Three: Care of Patients Before, During and After Operation in the Theatre.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="unit-objectives">Unit Objectives<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#unit-objectives" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>

<li>Describe the instruments used in a theatre</li>
